If your car is hugely underwater, you can turn it in, you can get another car. Uh, there's different things that we can help that way. Uh keep in mind that a co-barorrower is an issue because they are not filing bankruptcy with you and so they would still owe the debt. Um but you can protect them. In a chapter 13, we have a codebettor stay where there cannot be any negativity towards that other person, that co-debtor, whether it be collection actions and so forth. Um, and then what we do is we try to settle with that co-debtor that didn't file bankruptcy um so that they are not impaired, their credit's not impaired, their life is not impaired by the um student loan that maybe you went to college for. And so um we can often address private student loans. We can also file objections to proofs of claim in a chapter 13. We can object to the amounts claimed, the ownership, the statute of limitations, all kinds of things. Those orders will survive and they can be used in state court later to show your side of the story. You know, what it is that you really owe, uh why it is that they don't owe the they don't own the debt or they've waited too long to sue and they've um gone past the statute of limitations. Um for federal student loans, interestingly, there was a program announced. It's basically guidance uh November of 2022. Now, um since then, we've been able to do an attestation form in an adversary proceeding, which is basically another lawsuit filed in the bankruptcy. And we argue that this debt is an undue hardship because of all these reasons. One of the nice things is that that's a simplified way of doing it. We don't have to go through all the discovery and depositions and things like that. At least they have the option to do that if they want to, but usually they don't. And um what it is is this attestation form is sent to the department of ed then they make a recommendation. It goes down to the department of justice and eventually to the judge. Um what's interesting is that we are seeing that reactivated now. Um that program was initially uh began under the Trump 1.0 administration. Will it survive? Um normally I'm saying that guidance will not uh rule changes will not. Laws will. However, um this program appears to have the guard rails in place that we think that it is very likely to survive. Um it's actually restarted now just recently where folks are getting discharges, full discharges. And what's nice about the attestation program is that um it has these presumptions presumptions of discharge that didn't previously exist. If you're 65 years of age or age or older, if you um have an injury that impacts your ability to earn, not necessarily disability, but just an injury that impacts your ability to earn. If you didn't get a degree for which you went to school for, if you've been unemployed for a long period of time, those kinds of things can be presumptions now that they can be um opposed by the other side. But that's a step forward that we didn't used to have. So, we're seeing uh federal loan discharges, partial or full. We're seeing private loan discharges, uh, also settlements. You can reach a settlement on a private loan at a drastically reduced interest rate, a drastically reduced principal amount with a reasonable, affordable payment going forward, maybe for 20 years, but if it's $50 or $100, who really cares? It's no big deal. Um, versus the $750 to $1,000 payment that we're easily seeing outside of bankruptcy where maybe $5 is going to principal and you owe as much, if not more, after 10 years of repayment. That's what we're seeing outside of bankruptcy. So, don't forget about your bankruptcy options to address either federal or or private student loans. Even though, like I said, some of these programs have been in existence for 2, three years now, they haven't been utilized because of the pause, because of the soft start that didn't require payments for the first year after the pause ended. Um, those kinds of things now have started to stop. We've started to see um revisiting of collection that started again on May 5th, I think, of this year. We're starting to see garnishment, social security offsets that started again here this summer. We're starting to see interest acral begin again August 1st. Um these things are all going to lead to uh perhaps larger student loan payments. And if you're not happy with the options outside of bankruptcy, consider your options in bankruptcy. You might be pleasantly surprised. Um a lot of bankruptcy attorneys don't know that these things exist. So, be sure to contact someone who's knowledgeable about student loans um to either get involved with the bankruptcy, file the bankruptcy, or at least make some recommendations that you can then take to your bankruptcy attorney and suggest, hey, I know I can do these things. - The (Ir)rationality of the Dollar Auction: Kind: captions Language: en how much would you pay for a dollar surely it's no more than 99 cents right anything more would be irrational and most people understand this but there's a game where people end up paying as much as three to five dollars for a single dollar bill it's called the dollar auction game and it was devised by martin shubik an economist at yale the game is simple enough there aren't any tricks and players know the rules of the game from the start people end up paying absurd amounts for a dollar only because of their own irrational behaviors but before we get into that we need to understand how the game works one person holds up a dollar bill to a group of people and says I'll auction this dollar off to the highest bidder the auction works like you'd expect it to the highest bidder wins the dollar and then pays their bid to the auctioneer but there's one key difference the second highest bidder must also pay their bid even though they get nothing in return so the key to remember here is the top two bidders have to pay though the second highest bidder gets nothing and the highest bidder wins the dollar the game usually starts off with someone bidding five cents because why not five cents per dollar is a great deal then somebody else realizes that they too could get a dollar at a bargain so they make a bid for 10 cents once people enter the game it's usually not until the bids reached the 50 cent mark that players begin to see the game's logic here's what it looks like let's say player a bids 50 cents and player B bids 55 cents if player a quits they're out 50 Cent's gone but if they bid 60 cents they could win the dollar and walk away with a 40 cent profit here's the issue if player a raises their bid to 60 cents then player B finds themself at the exact same crossroads if they quit they're out 55 cents but if they were to bid up to 65 cents then they could win the dollar and walk away with a 35 cent profit this logic drives each player to continue bidding even past the dollar threshold where people begin to bid more than a dollar for a dollar at this point everyone realizes they've entered a full-blown war of attrition but they don't see how to get out of it think about it like this if player B's bid is at one dollar and five cents and lays bid is at $1 player B wins the auction but they paid a dollar and five cents to get the dollar so they've actually lost five cents overall meanwhile player a faces the sad reality that they'll have to pay a dollar and but they could soften the blow if they chose to bid up to $1.00 in ten cents then they would only lose ten cents overall rather than a dollar the difficulty here is that the line between a rational decision and an irrational one gets muddied bidding more than a dollar for a dollar isn't rational but choosing to extend the game so that you only lose ten cents rather than a dollar isn't exactly the poster child of rationality either it's difficult to tell which decision to make when neither is clearly the rational choice for time decision-making often follows pure dollar auction logic as well a government might continue to escalate commitment in hopes of winning the war but war leads to increasingly severe losses real losses of human lives are both sides in the end the winner is really just the smaller loser of the two the dollar auction ends the same way the winner of the auction gets the dollar but they're a winner in name only as they've actually lost money it's important to remember that each player began the game making rational decisions they saw an opportunity to gain a dollar at a bargain but as they pursued their own self-interest they fell into an escalation trap as the game continues it gets harder and harder to quit but the game will never end unless someone finally decides that enough is enough and they quit you might think that this is a silly game and you'd be right but this game works and it works because of human nature as humans we don't like losses and we're hopeful creatures in this game there's always a chance to improve our position and there's always a chance to win the dollar the issue is that it's temporary we might improve our position this round but it's going to worsen each time we face the decision to continue or quit there are a few lessons that we can take away from the dollar auction and the first is the line between a rational and an irrational decision is not always clear and I love this because that's how life is very few things present themselves as no-brainer decisions most of the time walking a tightrope of uncertainty we might have a chance to improve our position but there's also a chance we could worsen it and the better choice is not always crystal clear the second lesson is thinking strategically that's being able to see more than one step ahead is a valuable skill you might be able to exploit something in the short run but that same strategy might end up hurting you in the long run just like we saw in the game if you could have seen the consequences of winning the dollar auction from the start you may have never entered the game in the first place the third lesson is that sometimes your best strategy is to quit even if it means suffering a loss this conclusion is very similar to the one made in my sunk cost fallacy video though it might not feel good to quit sometimes it's the best decision we can make [Music]

Christy's inaugural auction dedicated to AI generated art entitled augmented intelligence unfolded amid a fervor of debate that underscores the deeper complexities inherent in the integration of artificial intelligence into artistic practices. Nearly a decade ago, while digital art was carving its niche, we embarked on a mission to challenge the traditional paradigms of art through inclusivity and innovation. Thus, we find it essential to explore the implications of this recent auction, dissect the controversies surrounding it, and articulate our perspective as champions of both artistry and technological advancement. Christ's augmented intelligence auction concluded with tangible results. $728,784 amassed and nearly half of the bidders identifying as millennials or Gen Z, a demographic that signals a significant shift in the art market. The auction houses projection of $600,000 was exceeded, boyed by eager participation from younger collectors and a notable 82% sell-through rate. High grossing lots like graphic anidals, machine hallucinations, ISS dreams, a fetched extraordinary prices prompting conversations about the valuation of AI art in contemporary culture. This substantial sum, however, did not come without contention. The auction was met with fierce opposition from nearly 4,000 artists who signed an open letter decrying the event as a perpetuation of exploitation within the art community. They argued that AI generated works too often rely on data sets sourced from existing human creations without consent or fair compensation. The crux of the debate surfaces as we must define not only the value of art but also the ethical standards that govern its creation. At the place to be gallery, we recognize the fears that many artists harbor regarding the emergence of AI in the art world. This sentiment can be distilled into three central themes. The potential erasure of human creativity. concerns regarding originality and authorship and the commodification of art itself. Artists have long cultivated their unique voices and visions, pouring countless hours into the development of their craftsmanship. The intrusion of AI into this sacred process can feel like a threat, an implication that creativity can be replicated by algorithms devoid of emotion or lived experience. This fear is valid. Art has always held profound value as a reflection of human experience. Yet it is crucial to differentiate between replacement and enhancement. We believe that technology should act as a collaborator in the artistic process, widening the horizon of what art can be rather than diminishing its core values. The question of originality looms large over AI art. When AI systems generate artworks through the analysis and recombination of existing pieces, the boundaries of authorship blur, who after all is the creator, the programmer, the data set, or the machine itself? As advocates for artists rights, we recognize that clarifying and safeguarding artistic ownership is paramount. However, we also assert that the creative communities can coexist with AI. Originality can emerge in new and unexpected forms when human artists utilize technological tools intentionally and ethically. The commodification of art is not a new phenomenon. However, the pace at which AI generated art is gaining traction can feel alarming. Collectible art has often shifted along the lines of trends and AI arts burgeoning market poses both opportunities and challenges. While we celebrate the democratization of art and collector access, we caution against allowing art to be reduced to mere transactions devoid of meaning or connection. The auction houses defense of AI as a legitimate tool that enriches artistic practice resonates with our own values at the place to be gallery. Nicole Sales Giles of Christy's notes that the participating artists are recognized figures within multiddisciplinary practices, diluting the criticism aimed at AI generated art. The artists behind these works blend traditional artistry with digital innovation, employing AI to expand their creative toolkit. Art history demonstrates numerous instances where tools, whether oil paint, photography or installation, have redefined artistic expression. Similarly, AI can serve as a contemporary brush, offering layers of complexity and resonance to artistic endeavors. Artists can harness AI's algorithmic capabilities, pushing boundaries and immersing viewers in new experiences that are otherwise unimaginable. We understand the importance of exploring these forward-thinking avenues. Despite the accolades granted to the innovators of AI generated works, ethical discourse must remain part of our collective journey within the art world. The principles of consent and compensation must govern the data sets used. As artists embrace AI technologies, there remains an ethical imperative to ensure that the foundations upon which these digital creations rest are built on respect and collaboration. Through education, dialogue, and transparent practices, we can ensure a harmonious coexistence between human artists and AI. At the place to be gallery, we actively engage artists in discussions surrounding tools that harness AI, ensuring they are equipped with knowledge of ethical data sourcing and creativity enhancing technologies. We urge institutions, galleries, and indeed the entire art community to forge alliances with artists, platforms, and developers that prioritize ethical frameworks as we advance into new artistic territories. This call to collaboration resonates with our vision. A space where artists feel both empowered and supported in the face of technological transformations. AI's role in art extends beyond mere creation. It triggers discussions about culture, identity, and society at large. As the boundaries between technology and humanity become further entwined, we must examine how AI reflects contemporary values and how art can interrogate the implications of a world increasingly dominated by artificial intelligence. The place to be gallery embraces these dialogues, offering exhibitions that explore the intersection of art, technology, and social commentary. The rise of AI generated art may evoke questions regarding authenticity in a world where clever algorithms can mimic human style. However, authenticity has never been a fixed concept in the art world. The value of an artwork often resides in the narrative it carries, be it the artist's lived experience or conveyed message. We advocate for a nuanced understanding of authenticity that embraces both traditional craftsmanship and innovative methodologies. The richness of our artistic landscape is imbued with diverse narratives that defy singular definitions. The influx of generative art and AI introduces new avenues for artistic expression and engagement. As young collectors flock to auctions like Christies, we witness a democratization of art, one shaped by nontraditional creators and emerging technologies. We celebrate the growing diversity within the art market, amplifying voices that have historically been marginalized. This evolution signals a bright future for art where traditional barriers subside, allowing new forms of creativity and expression to flourish. In closing, while the augmented intelligence auction at Christy's has ignited debate, we must recognize that the integration of AI into the art world presents an opportunity for growth and exploration rather than an end point. The place to be gallery stands firm in our commitment to supporting artists as they traverse the evolving landscape of contemporary creativity, ensuring their voices are heard and their rights protected. As we confront the dilemmas posed by AI, we welcome conversations surrounding ethics, authorship, and authenticity that underline our service to the artistic community. In this age of unprecedented transformation, it is within these discussions that we will chart a course towards a more equitable and enlightened future, one where technology and artistry coexist in harmony. Here at the place to be gallery, we invite you to engage with us as we navigate these uncharted waters together, forging a path towards innovative expressions that challenge us to rethink the future of art. Let us celebrate artistry in all its forms, human and machine alike, paving new avenues for inspiration while honoring the rich tapestry of creativity intrinsic to the human experience. The journey continues, and together we can facilitate an art world replete with endless possibilities.

- Why This Rare Van Gogh Isn’t Just a Work of Art, it's Work AS Art | Sotheby’s: Kind: captions Language: en [music] >> This Van Gogh isn't just a work of art, it's work as art. Vincent Van Gogh's La Moisson en Provence, or The Harvest in Provence, [music] is a beautiful watercolor that he created in June of 1888. He shows [music] the broad expanse of the plain with Alpilles mountains in the background. There's a ruined abbey at the upper left, but this is not just a landscape, [music] it's also a depiction of human labor. This is the harvest, he's depicting one of the most important moments in the agricultural calendar. There is a man on top of a filled cart who's about to bale hay. [music] There's another horse and cart moving in the background. There's a man scything the harvest and two women walking back into [music] the expanse of the plains. And of course in the center holding all of this together [music] is a blue cart that is seemingly imbued with the personality of the artist. This is a painting about the everyday. Most high art at this time and throughout the 18th [music] and 19th centuries was about history painting, biblical scenes, mythology. The concept of showing things as they were, or your impression of something and how it was, it was very revolutionary. Van Gogh's point [music] is that before there is art, there is bread. There is the blade of the harvester, there is the pitchfork of the farmer, the labor of the cart horse. If you don't have a stable [music] base of food, you don't have civilization, and you certainly don't have someone who can take the time to show what they're doing in [music] watercolor. When he was in Arles, he created some of his most famous compositions. He created Starry Night, [music] he created The Night Cafe, he created the first of his sunflower still lifes. It's where he lived with Gauguin in the famous yellow [music] house, it's where he cut off his ear. This was where Van Gogh took [music] everything he had learned from his time in Paris, where he had seen all the avant-garde had to offer. Arles completely absorbed him, the huge clouds, the gorgeous [music] landscape, the bright hot colors. It was here he found where Cézanne came from. He was always intrigued by Cézanne, but suddenly Cézanne made sense. He started to create his own vocabulary. He created what we think of as Van Gogh. Van Gogh's watercolors are immensely rare [music] and immensely special. It's hard to describe just how rare they are. When you think of Van Gogh, you're thinking there aren't that many, and [music] it's true. He was not a hugely productive artist, we're not talking about Picasso who has tens of thousands of works of art. But Van Gogh was primarily two things, a draftsman and a painter. And his watercolor combines [music] those two qualities. What makes this watercolor so special is not just the way he outlines [music] the figures in pen, but that he then comes back over the pen with washes [music] and different colorations of watercolor to create the movement and breath and [music] air of this scene. You know he made this outside. According to the Van Gogh Museum, he painted this one in one session, and because of that there's an inherent honesty to it. [music] And honesty is really the quality of Van Gogh's work that makes him so appealing. His work is universal, it's for everyone, and it depicts [music] everyone. And that was very important to him. That honesty is what attracts [music] us to Van Gogh and what attracted Van Gogh to the laborer. This is really Van Gogh experiencing [music] the landscape. This is what he sees, what he feels, and it is what he will use to create everything after it.

So the market is strong and obviously we're still standing just in front of a big sales season coming up and we'll see what that season will bring, but at least based on the previous data we could see how this market has been up to now. It's interesting to look at the different segments. We can see that, for example, the young contemporary market has done particularly well in the last two years, where we are suddenly starting to see the young generation of artists coming into the 1 million segment, which I think if, Georgina, if we went back 10 years ago, we probably wouldn't see that many 1 million lots by young artists. So there's also a whole... That's not only about established post-war and contemporary, but also young artists are now entering into this particular market, which may be something that can be discussed later on in the panel. The post-war market has been strong, again, fueled by several artists, but one of them is being in the Warhol market. That's been particularly strong in the last couple of years. And I think this drives us into other things which I think is interesting about this report is that by not only looking at the genres but looking at the, in this case, gender, looking at... It's not, not parity yet, but I think the fact that we are starting to see more women artists selling art works in the 1 million plus, it's definitely encouraging. We have seen the share of this growing from 8.2% in 2018 to about 13.9 in 2022. So it's it going the right direction. Sales has more than doubled from about 222 million to about 462 million during this period. This is not something that we only see in the 1 million plus market, but we see that across the market that there is gradual more presence and representation of women artists in the art market, which is a positive sign. But it's also good to see that this is not only happening at the maybe lower to mid-segment but also at the top end. Which, as I mentioned earlier, I think acts as a bellwether and sets the precedence for what's going to trickle down into other markets. The last bit is generational shift and I think this is an important part of the market right now. But the other thing which I think is important, one thing is just supply side, but the other thing is the buy side. And here we're talking not the lower end the market, we're talking the 1 million plus market which has grown basically from 7% to 16% in 2022. - The formula for selling a million-dollar work of art: Kind: captions Language: en In September of 2008, something unheard of happened in the art world. A British artist, Damien Hirst, took 223 pieces of his new work to Sotheby’s auction house and sold every single piece. I’ll start the bidding here at £2,500,000. It was a 2 day event, and the total sale was about $200 million. It broke the record for single artist auction of $20 million back in 1993. Hirst’s work&nbsp;included things like this zebra, this unicorn, and this painting made from butterflies. So how did he –&nbsp;how do artists&nbsp;– do this? Well for the most part, the artists aren't the ones behind it. Okay, so obviously money isn’t the only value that defines a work of art, and&nbsp;who knows how history will remember this unicorn. But order to sell a million-dollar artwork, you need strong market value– and you need extremely high demand. And a ton of work goes into creating that. As Don Thompson describes in his book, the formula for art pricing goes something like this. The bigger the work, generally the more expensive it is. But, the biggest variable is the reputation of the artist. Sometimes you’re world-famous, and sometimes you’re not. What? But when a new artist steps into the art market, the reputation of the artist heavily relies on the name of the dealer. This shark by Damien Hirst is a good example. Hirst first began working with an art industry giant, Charles Saatchi, in the 1990s. Saatchi commissioned Hirst to make anything he wanted for £50,000 after seeing this cow’s head at a show shortly after Hirst’s graduation in 1990. Hirst bought a shark for £6000 from an Australian fisherman and created this, injecting it with tons of formaldehyde. Later in 2004, it was sold for $12 million to a billionaire hedge fund manager, Steve Cohen. It was roughly 130 times the original price but it makes sense considering Saatchi’s reputation. And it makes more sense when you think about how it was bought –&nbsp;dealers can use selective information to get potential buyers to pay more. Hirst’s huge auction I mentioned before… For dealer-sold work, everything is private, including the prices, which gives dealers the upper hand in pricing. In 1988, New York City tried to ban this by reinforcing the Truth in Pricing law, and galleries fought back HARD, paying fines and protesting saying that showing prices will be “getting in the way of the enjoyment of the exhibition.” By keeping the price private, art dealers can rely more on their reputation to make the artwork feel more valuable to the buyer. Outside this equation, the basic laws of economics also apply. The next step of operations for the dealer is creating scarcity. In 1999, when Jenny Saville, a new emerging British artist became affiliated with Charles Saatchi, he convinced her to cut her work down to only 6 paintings per year. He sold them for $100,000 each. So what does this all result to? According to Artnet, the estimated size of the art market was $64 billion in 2015. And&nbsp;market is growing outside of traditional sales of galleries and auction houses. This chart shows the art world might be learning the lesson Saatchi taught Jenny Savile –the total value of the art that’s being sold is growing faster than the number of pieces. Sell less of it, for more. But to sell that million-dollar artwork, you’ll need reputation bigger than Hirst’s, or Charles Saatchi’s. The dealer model still dominates the fine art world, but for the rest of us, selling art online has never been easier. The prices are open and it’s accessible for a broad group of people. And for one thing, now you know where to start: think big.

precious and highly secured artwork although this scene is a bit unrealistic these free ports do exist and today we'll take a closer look at how they enable the ultra wealthy to avoid taxes on their prized art collections in countries like Switzerland Luxembourg and Singapore freeports are economic free zones where billionaires can store their valuable assets including art operating outside traditional custom zones these free ports allow for the temporary storage of goods without incurring taxes or duties they were initially designed to facilitate trade by offering a tax-free stopover for goods in transit but over time people saw the potential in these spaces to act as a storage facility for the wealthy and there's no bigger or better storage facility in the world than the Geneva Freeport home to the largest art collection in the world the Geneva Freeport located in Switzerland is one of the most famous freeports in the world it was established in 1888 and has evolved into a massive high security storage facility with around 1.2 million square feet of storage space the Freeport is home to an estimated 100 billion dollars worth of art making it one of the world's largest and most valuable art repositories but what makes the Geneva Freeport so attractive to art collectors one a strategic location in Switzerland offers political stability keeping its assets safe in times of global uncertainty two the Swiss government's strict privacy laws protect the identity of the freeport's clients allowing them to remain anonymous and three the tax-free environment helps collectors avoid paying taxes on their art collections allowing them to store their assets indefinitely Or sell them without incurring additional costs the Freeport serves as a preferred location for businesses as a Transit Zone Proprietors aren't subject to taxes as long as their merchandise remains on the premises like any other Freeport the regulations governing Geneva's Freeport are lenient the identities of buyers and sellers the items being traded and the prices all remain undisclosed creating the perfect environment for discreet Art Market sales and potentially fraudulent dealings artwork can be bought and sold numerous times without ever leaving the confines of the Freeport and many such transactions have managed to slip under the radar of the Customs Administration but the Geneva Freeport isn't just known for its role in art storage it's also been linked to multiple scandals and controversies imagine cruising down a picturesque Italian Highway when out of the blue a car accident up ahead Alters the course of history this exact scenario unfolded in 1995 when an ex-policeman's vehicular mishap exposed an international network of looted Antiquities connected to Italian art dealer Giacomo Medici Giacomo had hidden thousands of stolen Roman and Etruscan artifacts in his vault at the Geneva Freeport with some even being sold to famous museums the Freeport also housed two paintings by Vincent van Gogh and Claude Monet stolen in separate heists in 2008 and 2010. it hardly ends there in 2003 Swiss authorities discovered 290 looted Egyptian Antiquities including mummies in a vault at the Geneva Freeport these scandals have raised concerns about the lack of transparency and regulation in freeports leading to calls for stricter controls and increased oversight Medici was sentenced to jail for seven years and fined 10 million euros in 2004 but the freeport's used as a tax Haven has not stopped following these incidents Geneva Freeport has evolved to improve traceability and transparency to combat illicit activities within its walls although some scandals have tarnished its reputation Geneva Freeport continues to provide discretion for legitimate art transactions while upholding a stable political and legal environment as we've seen the fight against fraud and money laundering in the art world is ongoing but progress is being made to protect the   1. @reddashgames7550 [17 likes]: It’s clear that “Clear Glasses” has no idea what they’re talking about. Tossing illustrators under the bus for supposedly lacking creativity? Have they even looked at illustrators’ work?

As someone barely scraping by as an artist, I’ve seen illustrators constantly dismissed. Even in school, I failed art basically for not being a ‘real artist’ aka: being an illustrator, while so-called “real artists” used the same methods and techniques fitting in themes and feels, they say they want to be different yet they seem to be more interested in pleasing investors and those upper class folks that can afford them (they rarely sell much hence prices, that or shady stuff going on). Meanwhile, I was creating entire worlds—fantastical creatures, unique cultures, and deep stories that explore love, pain, and suffering—yet somehow, I was considered less creative than someone splattering paint on a canvas.

That’s not to say abstract or conceptual art has no value, but let’s be real: many of these artists receive outside funding, grants, or sheer luck. Meanwhile, illustrators are portrayed as the privileged ones? For over a century, the art world has stamped on illustrators. Just look at all the products in your home—notice the lack of credit for the artists behind them? Illustrators aren’t seen as individuals; they’re treated as anonymous workers for corporations, despite each of them having their own hopes, dreams, and artistic perspectives that shape their work.

And yet, some people deliberately misrepresent illustrators, claiming to defend “small creators” while actually siding with multi-million-dollar companies that have no respect for them. AI art is the perfect example of this. Most AI models exist by ripping off illustrators—scraping their work without consent, reducing them to nothing more than data points for a machine to regurgitate their style. These companies aren’t supporting artists; they’re using their work, their years of effort, and their creativity to train models that replace them while profiting off their stolen labor. And somehow, illustrators are still the ones being accused of lacking originality?

This entire argument falls apart when you realize that AI art wouldn’t even exist without illustrators. It’s built on their backs, trained on their work, and yet, instead of being credited or compensated, they’re dismissed as if they’re the problem. Either people don’t understand this, or they’re actively pushing corporate propaganda to justify devaluing real artists even further.


In short… I’m tired of ai junk, it’s so.. tiring. 
But as they say, cats out the bag now… so unsure what can even be done about this.

  19. @marjanm5330: Trying to decide whether a new technology-form constitutes “art” is a discussion we had many times before (see in the last 150 years: photography, film, avant-garde, impressionism, modernism, primitive art, pop-music, pop-art etc etc).I doubt that there is a universal agreement/understanding, of what constitutes art. It is linked to time, context, technology, economy, psychology, social and cultural sensitivities, impacting the hierarchy of aesthetic/moral/financial/conceptual values that we attribute.

In this case, of course we are not just discussing “what is art” but also “what is AI”. Another impossible question, especially since it doesn’t really exist (yet).

The actual idea, to-date, is that it is a new technological tool for people (artists) to generate images or sound. With a human generated concept and a human executed editor-ship (so to speak). A modern collage-making machine.That is not really AI. 
Although we might like to think it. 

The ultimate idea of AI is that it is completely artificial intelligence. A new intelligence, devoid of humanity (or far ahead of what humans could accomplish), that a) cracks the code to understanding what art is, (thereby assuming that such an ultimate truth might exist, which is quite unlikely imo) and b) manages to faultlessly execute such pieces that make that code visible or audible. A truly AI art-generator would make art, not for humans, but for itself or other AI-entities. 

Personally I think art is fundamentally an organic thing. Patterns might be beautiful, and useful, but it is in the interruption of the pattern that lies the real attraction, the unexpected, the individual, the thing that is irreproducible, the soul. As humans, we have a basic understanding, a basic consciousness, of this. That is why we long to be creative. To understand the patterns and the chaos and bend them to our will (or sensitivities or abilities) so we may capture beauty or truth. A truth that is collective AND personal. It is more in this sphere, imo,
that the real meaning or art lies, which is why this discussion is - whilst fascinating - only useful as a tool to get closer to knowing what art is to us and totally pointless as regards AI.

- 10 Wardrobe Items I Regret Buying: Poor Menswear Choices (and Their Stories): Kind: captions Language: en Welcome back to the Gentleman's Gazette. In today's video, we'll discuss ten things in my wardrobe that were regrettable purchases, why I bought them, and what I've learned since then. If you haven't seen Rafael's video&nbsp; on 20 things he regrets buying, I'd recommend taking a look. You'll probably learn some things not only about men's style but also about money management. Today's video covers similar ground for items I've purchased, whether that be individual garments or collections of things with one key difference from Rafael's video, I don't actually regret buying all of them. You'll see what I mean by this in a moment but to get things rolling, let's start with 6 things that I definitely do regret buying. First is clinical strength antiperspirant and antiperspirant in general. As I've mentioned elsewhere on the channel, I have a condition called Hyperhidrosis, which is marked by excessive perspiration. Sparing all of the details for the sake of modesty, what's important here is that over the years, I've tried numerous products to try to minimize perspiration across my body when it comes to my underarms. I used a clinical strength antiperspirant for many years but because my perspiration was so persistent, all I would end up with was an unpleasant sludge under my arms after usually just a few hours. Also because aluminum is a key ingredient in many antiperspirants serving to block the pores, it can corrode over time and this more than bacteria or the sweat itself is the thing that chiefly causes underarm stains on shirts; and with all the aluminum that was in that antiperspirant, there was even a time or two that I set off an airport metal detector. I'm not kidding. About two years ago, I decided that if these antiperspirants weren't doing a good job of stopping my sweating, and if they were causing these inconveniences besides, I might as well stop fighting the root cause. Since then I've switched to non-antiperspirant deodorant and while I do still sweat quite a bit and may seek other medical solutions in the future for it, I have found that not using antiperspirant to fight my underarm sweating has left me feeling better overall. Next up is two-in-one shampoo and conditioner as we've previously mentioned in our video on 15 healthy hair tips which you can find here, There are a number of reasons not to buy combination shampoo conditioners. For a start, shampoo is something that can actually do more harm than good to your hair if used improperly. Cheap shampoo, though it is economical, is often full of ingredients that are collectively known as sulfates. While these detergents can help to make the product sudsy, they also deprive your hair of its natural oils, leaving it looking dry and brittle. To avoid these effects, you can look for a low sulfate or sulfate-free shampoo which cleans without removing the oil completely. Second and perhaps more importantly, You actually don't need to use shampoo every time you shower. Constant shampooing even with sulfate-free products can strip your scalp of natural oils and will definitely dry out your hair even if it's already somewhat dry, to begin with. Therefore most barbers and stylists will recommend that while you should rinse your hair every time you shower, you should only use shampoo one to two times a week. What you should use more often and even daily then is conditioner, which helps to protect and moisturize your hair. All that to say these combinations or two-in-one products are essentially making each of their components these shampoo and conditioner less effective on both fronts, and it goes without saying here for any of those three-in-one combination products that also include body wash, stay away. I used a two-in-one product for years but since switching to separate shampoo and conditioner about a year ago, I've noticed that my hair feels noticeably healthier. I only shampoo about once or twice a week, usually following whenever I've used a hair product. I haven't written off liquid products entirely and switched to bar soap as Raphael has, but it may be something to consider in the future. My next regrettable purchase is this grey suit. I purchased the suit I'm wearing in today's video seven years ago at the age of 18, and it was my first true suit. More on what I mean by that, later. The trousers came unfinished so having to go to an alterations tailor to get them hemmed made me feel like I was getting the suit to fit just right. Little did I know at the time though that there are actually many more facets to getting a suit to fit you well. Yes, the construction and materials of this suit are also quite cheap which made for a low price point that was attractive to a cash-strapped student like myself but as we've said many times before, cheap materials are secondary to a proper fit. But of course, I readily admit that this suit is far from properly fitting on me. The jacket is much too big in the torso for one thing, so it looks like I'm practically swimming in fabric. The trouser legs are also too wide for my build, but because the fabric is light, there isn't any attractive drape to them, just ugly wrinkling. I could go on but suffice to say that this suit definitely doesn't meet our fit criteria. I probably could try to have it tailored further to get it to fit better, but I think a better choice would be just to find a suit that fits better from the outset and is of a bit higher quality. So, stay tuned for that in the future. Where did I get this suit, you asked? The answer is the now ever-present force in so many of our lives -- amazon.com And that brings me to my next pick for today which is several of the other garments I've bought or tried to buy from that website. From a sport coat that was too boxy on me and of low quality to trousers that were far too baggy, (sensing a pattern here?) also too cheap ties, I've realized especially since joining the Gentleman's Gazette that buying clothing sight unseen from a cheap, mass-market retailer instead of a dedicated menswear outlet, means you do get what you pay for, which is usually disappointing results. Speaking of ties, the next item on the list is my overly shiny, satin silk or synthetic ties in my wardrobe. Some of these were purchased from Amazon, such as the bow tie I'm wearing here today and others were purchased in person from stores, but the common denominator here is that they're blindingly shiny finish, and often bright loud colors make them look like cheap, low-quality ties almost immediately. They were at least tolerable for a young man just getting into menswear, but since learning about the hallmarks of a quality tie, especially like those from Fort Belvedere, these shiny ties don't see much use for me anymore, outside perhaps of a Halloween costume. Similarly cheap and low-quality dress shoes are my next item here. Before joining the Gentleman's Gazette, I've viewed dress shoes in a way that's similar to many men out there which is to say that in today's fast fashion, society shoes should be purchased for a reasonable price of around $100 or so, worn until the materials wear out and then thrown away and replaced. Some examples would include the pair of loafers you're seeing here and the Darby shoes I'm wearing in today's video. The truth about men's dress shoes though is that if you're willing to spend a bit more at the outset, say something in the two to four hundred dollar range you're going to be able to find shoes that are made of higher quality materials and with better construction. Take for example, the Park Avenue from Allen Edmonds. For a sticker price of $395 but often on sale for less, you'll be getting a shoe made from quality leather in both the upper and the sole, rather than a cheap shoe with a cheap leather upper, and the sole made from rubber plastic or particleboard. And also, of note here is that shoes with a Goodyear welt or a blake stitch can be re-soled by a cobbler. So, if you maintain the upper properly, they can be worn for years, if not, decades. Meanwhile, these cheap shoes had their heel blocks worn down in around a year's time and because the soles are just glued on rather than stitched, they can't reasonably be repaired. In addition, the cheap leather uppers are showing discoloration in an unattractive way and because the shoes are slightly too big on me to begin with, they're also wrinkling unattractively. So, those are the six things that I truly regret buying. Next up are four things that I could regret but don't, at least not entirely. The next four picks that round out my list today are things that could be considered regrettable but for one reason or another, I'm still glad that I bought them. First up is my fairly extensive collection of Charles Tyrwhitt shirts, such as the light blue one I'm wearing in today's video. Why would these be considered regrettable? Well, in one word, the answer is fit. All of these shirts are much too big on me in both the torso and the arms. Because they were my first foray into online made-to-measure, I selected slim-fit not knowing that for someone as light and thin as I am, this would still be much too big on my frame. Also, I started collecting these shirts before joining the Gentleman's Gazette, so I didn't have a complete picture of how a dress shirt should actually fit. Why don't I regret buying them then? Well as I said they were my first experience with made-to-measure and I appreciated going through the process and having something that was made for me, even if they aren't true bespoke. Furthermore, I now most often wear them underneath jackets or sweaters, so their too large fit is somewhat obscured, and therefore they still work for me for the time being as I look toward the future to upgrade my shirt wardrobe. And as I've mentioned before, I do still get plenty of compliments on them as well. Finally here learning that something doesn't fit well on you is still learning. Style should be an ever-evolving journey and these shirts have played an important part in mine. Also, the difference between these and something like my gray suit is that while I can find ways to still make the shirts look reasonably good, the fit of this suit is immediately apparent, so it's really a no-go for me at this point in time. Speaking of which you'll recall that toward the beginning of the video, I mentioned that my gray suit was the first true suit that I owned. This is because the first suit that I had wasn't really a suit at all. What do I mean by this? In high school, I participated in Forensics which is to say Speech and Debate teams. At least at that time, the standard uniform for men was a black suit. Being young, inexperienced in the ways of menswear, and on a budget, I simply picked up a black jacket from a department store and paired it with a pair of black trousers that I'd already had for years. Fortunately, the dyes and weaves of the two garments were similar enough, that to the casual observer, they actually did appear to match. But as we've discussed numerous other times on the channel, a suit is made from a jacket and trousers as well as an optional waistcoat that are all cut from the same fabric. As with the gray suit, the black jacket was much too baggy on me. Why don't I regret this ensemble then? The answer is because it made me feel confident and in control, as a suit ideally should. It wasn't strictly correct by the guidelines of menswear, but it was for me a suit of armor that helped me feel more comfortable giving speeches in front of people, something with which I had struggled earlier in childhood. In fact, if I hadn't joined these Speech and Debate teams in high school, I probably wouldn't have been confident enough to eventually find my way to on-camera speaking, or joining the Gentleman's Gazette. I'm not saying that this black suit was directly responsible for this, but it did probably play a small part. Another black item next, the black wool felt Fedora you're seeing here. Around the same time as my purchase of my black suit, I was a teenager just getting into the world of menswear. I really didn't know much about fit yet, that of course would take a while, but I knew that I wanted a hat to go with my suit as such. I found a black wool felt Fedora online. Now, it wasn't a cheap trilby bought from Target, which were quite popular around 2011 or so, but in fact a genuine Fedora purchased from a hatter and while it was pretty soft in construction, black wasn't a flattering or versatile color for me and the brim was too wide for my face, this hat did still give me confidence. I wore it often with that black suit in high school and even with non-matching outerwear for my first few years of college. Once I started looking around for vintage Fedoras on eBay and in secondhand stores, however, I started building up my current collection and this hat stayed on the rack. As you can see though, I still haven't gotten rid of the old hat yet, because it holds a special place in my wardrobe. And who knows, maybe for ultra-casual outfits or doing things outdoors where I'd still like to be wearing a traditional hat, and black works in my wardrobe, it could still have a place. To round out my list today, I've got one more cheap black item, a pre-tied bow tie. As regular viewers are no doubt aware, we here at the Gentleman's Gazette recommend that a man learn to tie his own bowtie whenever possible. A pre-tied bow tie is cold and sterile in its perfection and it immediately gives off a juvenile, unprofessional look. But in fairness, I first purchased a pre-tied bow tie at the age of 15, when I was still a juvenile and a non-professional. I needed it for a makeshift tuxedo of sorts, so I wore it along with a borrowed black jacket and those trusty black trousers, to serve as master of ceremonies for a school concert in the spring of 2009. My lack of regret for purchasing this bowtie comes from the fact that it did make me feel confident in front of a crowd honing my skills as a presenter for really the first time, even before later joining the speech team. There's my list for today then. Six items I can honestly say I do regret buying, and for that while objectively regrettable, have played an important part in my own style journey. And we would not have seen that 20 years ago. The best indicator of what industry people will come from is what industry is making the most money. We sell a luxury object for a lot of money, so if it's bankers who are making a lot of money, our audience base will grow in the banking community. If it's a great time, Protec, we'll have more tech people. If it's a great time in real estate. We'll have more real estate people. And the biggest driver of that then isn't just what people in this country are doing, but what is changing in the global economy. So we've seen enormous growth from all over Asia. But despite growing global concerns, the auction houses remain optimistic for the art market. The political climate in Europe and Brexit creates some trepidation. The political climate in China and Hong Kong create some trepidation. And the tariff disputes also create trepidation. That said, what we've seen is that works of art are seen as a stable, constant store of value. They're personally edifying to anybody who has them. They are good for the community when people buy them for museums or create museums. All else being in flux. It seems like an island of stability and calm in terms of people's desire to commune with works of art and to live with them. The world is generally a much richer place than it was a few years ago, and I think everybody sees that trend continuing. I have to say that the trend is for wealthy people to get even wealthier. The scale of the wealth in the hands of people now is breathtaking and they are collectors and they will be desperate to acquire iconic works of art.
